THE REPORTS BY THE JURIES: A FINDING AID FOR A PUBLICATION FROM THE GREAT EXHIBITION OF 1851 IN THE COLLECTION OF THE ART GALLERY OF ONTARIO by Jill Offenbeck, BFA, Ryerson University, Toronto, 2003

A Thesis Project Presented to Ryerson University and the Art Gallery of Ontario in partial fulfillment of the requirements for the degree of MASTER OF ARTS in the Program of Photographic Preservation and Collections Management

Toronto, Ontario, Canada, 2011 !Jill Offenbeck 2011

! I hereby declare that I am the sole author of this thesis or dissertation. I authorize Ryerson University to lend this thesis or dissertation to other institutions or individuals for the purpose of scholarly research.

I further authorize Ryerson University to reproduce this thesis or dissertation by photocopying or by other means, in total or in part, at the request of other institutions or individuals for the purpose of scholarly research.

The Reports by the Juries: A Finding Aid for a Publication from the Great Exhibition of 1851 in the Collection of the Art Gallery of Ontario Master of Arts, 2011 Jill Offenbeck Photographic Preservation and Collections Management Ryerson University Abstract

Produced by the Royal Commission for the Great Exhibition of 1851, the four-volume Reports by the Juries is illustrated with 154 salted paper prints from negatives by ClaudeMarie Ferrier (1811-1889) and Hugh Owen (1808-1897) and document the exhibits and the Crystal Palace building. 137 copies of the Reports were produced and distributed internationally to individuals and countries that participated in the exhibition. This presentation set, which also includes the Official Descriptive and Illustrated Catalogue, the Reports to the Crown, and a presentation set of exhibition medals, belonged to Sir William Crossman (1830-1901) of the Royal Engineers. A specially designed wooden cabinet houses the set. This thesis project is divided into two parts: Part I describes the process of researching and creating the finding aid; Part II is the finding aid itself, containing ten sections addressing historical background information, photographers' biographies, photographic processes, a complete illustrated catalogue of the photographs, an analysis of the photographs, provenance, and a preliminary list of other copies of the publication in public institutions.

!

#!

! Acknowledgements

I am appreciative of the continuous support of my advisor Professor David Harris, whose guidance and encouragement were crucial to the completion of this project. I am grateful to Maia-Mari Sutnik, Curator of Photographs, Special Projects, and Sophie Hackett, Assistant Curator of Photographs, at the Art Gallery of Ontario, for their kindness and generosity, as always. For access to the resources of the Robertson Davies Library, Massey College in the University of Toronto, I must thank P.J. MacDougall, Librarian, who patiently responded to my repeated requests. The list of libraries and institutions holding other copies of the Reports by the Juries could not have been compiled without the helpful assistance and prompt replies from the kind librarians, archivists, and collection specialists that I contacted around the world. I am grateful for the many professors and instructors I have had throughout my studies, at Ryerson University, George Eastman House, and the Art Gallery of Ontario, for providing helpful resources and much inspiration. I am indebted to my family and friends, who were there to make me laugh in the most important moments, and who provided endless encouragement.

Part I: Thesis Project
Introduction

The Reports by the Juries marked a very important moment within the history of photography. Illustrated with 154 salted paper prints, the seminal publication helped to establish photography as a viable medium in Great Britain.1 It was a monumental undertaking, and is considered one of the most ambitious projects of its kind to be completed up to that time2. The Reports by the Juries was produced to commemorate the Great Exhibition of the Works of Industry of All Nations (also known as the Great Exhibition, or more commonly, the Crystal Palace Exhibition), which took place in London, England from May to October 1851. It is an important record of the exhibition, and documents not only the extraordinary Crystal Palace building where the event was held, but also the wide range of objects and instruments that were put on display for the world to see. The four volumes of the Reports by the Juries form part of an eight volume official presentation set, of which 137 sets were produced and distributed internationally to those involved with the exhibition. The Art Gallery of Ontario (AGO) holds a complete set in its photography collection, which was received as a donation in 2007. This set originally belonged to Sir William Crossman (1830-1901), of the Corps of Royal

Engineers, who assisted with the organization of the Great Exhibition.3 Of particular note is the specially designed wooden cabinet that houses this set, likely the only one of its kind. This thesis project provides a finding aid for the Reports by the Juries for use by AGO staff, outside researchers, and students, and is divided into two parts. Part I briefly describes the process of creating the finding aid. The second and much larger part is the finding aid itself, and is conceived as a self-contained document; it includes ten sections, which focuses primarily on the photographs within the Reports. Contextual information is provided, such as the photographic processes used and biographies on the photographers. The complete illustrated catalogue of the photographs is a very significant portion of the finding aid. As a catalogue and visual record of this material has not been previously available, this will increase access to and interest in the photographs for future study.

! Literature Survey

!

!

While much has been written about the Great Exhibition of 1851, little exists on the Reports by the Juries and the photographs that appear within the volumes. This literature survey is divided into four sections, as related to my areas of research. First, I introduce the sources I consulted regarding constructing the finding aid; next, I indicate publications consulted concerning the history of the Great Exhibition of 1851; I then outline the resources pertaining to 19th-century photography; and finally, I describe the publications that specifically discuss the Reports by the Juries.

Finding Aids As my project takes the form of a finding aid, I began with research on the subject of finding aids. Gregory S. Hunter's 2003 publication Developing and Maintaining Practical Archives: A How-To-Do It Manual outlines the process of creating a successful finding aid in a step-by-step format. The text is written in basic terms, and this allows the reader to have a clear understanding of the goals and objectives that he or she must consider when creating a finding aid. Because there appears to be little information describing the photographs in the Reports by the Juries, one of my main goals was to include information in the finding aid that would allow researchers to have a clear description of each photograph and how they function within the volumes. For this, I consulted various resources related to cataloguing and describing collections, including Describing Archives: A Content Standard (DACS), a 2004 publication approved by the Society of American Archivists (SAA). Additionally, in the 2006 publication

!

'!

!

!

!

Photographs: Archival Care and Management by Mary Lynn Ritzenthaler and Diane Vogt-O'Connor and also published by the Society of American Archivists, the chapter "Description and Cataloging" clearly defines standards for describing photographs, including a specific section on finding aids. Finally, I found that examining other finding aids was helpful in determining the scope and content of such a document. I consulted findings aids that are currently in use at the George Eastman House, as well as a number created by previous students of the Photographic Preservation and Collections Management Program.4

The Great Exhibition To gain context and knowledge of the time period, various publications about the Great Exhibition of 1851 were consulted. Jeffrey A. Auerbach's 1999 book The Great Exhibition of 1851: A Nation on Display, and Hermione Hobhouse's 2002 publication The Crystal Palace and the Great Exhibition: Art, Science, and Productive Industry: A History of the Royal Commission for the Exhibition of 1851, both offered valuable historical information about the event. In particular, Hobhouse's publication presented a detailed history on the Royal Commission, the body responsible for creating the Reports by the Juries publication.5 The Official Descriptive and Illustrated Catalogue, which accompanies the Reports by the Juries, was an essential publication in providing !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!

information about how the Great Exhibition was structured, and the names of the exhibitors and the countries present. ! ! 19th-century Photography The Golden Age of British Photography 1839-1900, edited by Mark HaworthBooth, presents a chronological overview of the medium in that country, and in his introduction, Haworth-Booth situates the Great Exhibition as one of the most important events in the history of photography. Roger Taylor's 2007 catalogue Impressed by Light: British Photographs from Paper Negatives, 1840-1860 notes the importance of the exhibition in the history of and development of paper photography in England from the 1840s through to 1862. In particular, Taylor's chapter "The Great Exhibition of 1851" specifically describes how the Reports by the Juries played a key role in establishing photography as a viable medium, despite the complications that arose during the process of publication. Biographical information about the individuals involved with the Reports by the Juries, particularly Claude-Marie Ferrier, Hugh Owen, Robert Bingham and Nicolass Henneman, was drawn from a variety of sources. The 2008 publication Encyclopedia of Nineteenth-Century Photography, edited by John Hannavy, includes entries for nearly all those involved in the production of the Reports. Additionally, the biographical dictionary appearing in Taylor's Impressed By Light, and compiled by Larry J. Schaaf and Taylor, provided detailed entries for Owens, Henneman and W.H.F. Talbot. The 1970 text Bristol's Earliest Photographs, by Reece Winstone, is an informative source for its biographical information about the British calotypist Hugh Owen. John B. Cameron's

!

)!

!

!

!

2004 article "The Early Paper Stereographs of Claude-Marie Ferrier", provides an overview of Claude-Marie Ferrier's early life and work. A number of books proved helpful in researching the intricacies of the photographic processes used to create the negatives and positives in Reports by the Juries. In addition to William Crawford's Keepers of Light and Randall Webb and Martin Reed's Spirits of Salts: A Working Guide to Old Photographic Process, Bertrand Lavédrine's 2009 text Photographs of the Past: Process and Preservation was most valuable.

Reports by the Juries There are three key sources that deal specifically with the Reports by the Juries and the photographs in the publication. The earliest is Nancy Keeler's 1981 article "Illustrating the `Reports by the Juries' of the Great Exhibition of 1851; Talbot, Henneman, and Their Failed Commission" in which she provides a very thorough account of the controversy surrounding the creation of the photographs. The article focuses mostly on the dramatic correspondence between W.H. Fox Talbot and the Royal Commission, as they disagreed over photographic patent restrictions. Anthony Hamber's book A Higher Branch of the Art: Photographing the Fine Arts in England, 1839-1880, published in 1996, outlines the role that the Great Exhibition played in promoting and advancing photography. In particular, his chapter "Exhibiting Photography" provides an overview of the production and reception of the photographs for the Reports by the Juries. The most recent article is Saskia Asser's "A Handsome and Highly Finished

!

*!

!

!

!

Present': Foto's voor de juryrapporten van de Great Exhibition, 1851", from 2005.6 This article provides the most exhaustive study of the Reports by the Juries. The article discusses the production of the Reports by the Juries, including the conflict over the photographic patent, the involvement and work of the photographers, the printing of the photographs, and their reception. This article includes a comparative study of the photographs, a study that I also independently undertook. While these sources clearly indicate the importance of the Reports by the Juries to the history of photography, none includes a detailed inventory, description, and illustration of all the photographs in the volumes. The finding aid that I have completed fills this void, and compliments and extends the research on the Reports by the Juries by these three authors.

! Process and Methodology

!

!

The Reports by the Juries and the related three volume Official and Illustrated Catalogue are organized along different principles. The jury reports are divided into thirty classes, while the catalogues (which contain all the exhibitor information) are ordered by country, which at times becomes cumbersome to coordinate the information between the volumes. At the beginning of my research process, I spent many weeks systematically examining the catalogues and the Reports by the Juries. Aside from studying the photographs, I felt it was important to understand how the entire presentation set fit together, and what information was included in the entire published set. 7 It was also at this time that I consulted other relevant contemporary sources from the Great Exhibition, including documents and pamphlets published during the exhibition, most of which are held in the Robertson Davies Library, Massey College in University of Toronto (Robertson Davies Library) and the Thomas Fisher Rare Book Library. My initial task was to begin cataloguing the photographs in the AGO set. This coincided with visits to the Robertson Davies Library, which holds a copy of the Reports by the Juries, to begin a preliminary assessment of the photographs based on a comparative analysis. The comparative study proved invaluable and it informed further

I utilized resources from the Ryerson Library, the University of Toronto libraries, the Toronto Reference Library, and the Richard and Ronay Menschel Library at George Eastman House to complete my research for the written component of my finding aid. Saskia Asser's article "'A Handsome and Highly Finished Present': Foto's voor de juryrapporten van de Great Exhibition, 1851" was translated from Dutch to English by Bieke van der Mark for this project.8

The list of public institutions that currently hold a copy of the Reports by the Juries was compiled throughout the entire thesis project. I used various websites to locate copies, such as Worldcat and Amicus,9 and then contacted the institutions via email to determine whose copy they held. This was a very lengthy process, and while this list is not exhaustive, it does provide a starting point for further research (see section 9 of the finding aid).

%-!

! Conclusion

!

!

The Reports by the Juries was an extraordinary achievement, and was one of the first publications that successfully combined both text and original photographs. The Art Gallery of Ontario's copy, housed in its specially constructed cabinet, is a particularly exquisite copy of the complete presentation set. The accompanying finding aid includes important information related to the production of the photographs and relevant background information about the publication itself. The thesis project draws upon current literature about the Reports by the Juries, and adds to the scholarly research with the full catalogue and thumbnail reproduction of the photographs in the AGO's copy of the Reports by the Juries. The finding aid will be made available in the Marvin Gelber Print and Drawing Study Centre at the AGO, and through this thesis, will also be available to external researchers who can utilize the catalogue records when researching the Reports by the Juries, and the incredible photographs that are found within.

! Introductory Note

!

!

This finding aid has been created to provide historical and contextual background information related to the Reports by the Juries, including information about the Great Exhibition of 1851, the creation of the publication, the production of the photographs, and an extended analysis of the photography that illustrate the volumes. It also includes a complete catalogue of the 154 photographs that appear in the Art Gallery of Ontario (AGO) copy, including thumbnail reproductions of all the photographs. There are ten sections within this finding aid: 1. The Great Exhibition of 1851 This section provides background and historical information about the Great Exhibition of 1851, and provides a context for the production of the Reports by the Juries. 2. Reports by the Juries: Publication Information This section includes information pertaining to the creation and publishing of the Reports by the Juries. 3. Scope and Content: Physical Description of the Publication The publication and specially-designed cabinet are described in terms of their physical characteristics and components. 4. The Photographers and Their Biographies This section provides biographical information on Claude-Marie Ferrier (18111889) and Hugh Owen (1808-1897), the photographers who created the negatives, Robert Bingham (1824-1870) who is credited with printing most of the negatives. and Nicolass Henneman (1813-1898), who also played a role in the initial stages of the printing. ! %)!

!

!

!

5. Photographic Processes Used in the Publication This section outlines the photographic processes used to create the negatives and the prints for the Reports by the Juries, including paper negatives, albumenizedglass plate negatives, and salted paper prints. 6. Catalogue of the Photographs in the Reports by the Juries This section provides a complete catalogue for the 154 photographs that appear in the AGO's copy of the Reports by the Juries, including thumbnail reproductions of each photograph. It is prefaced by an introduction that explains the fields in the catalogue. A chart summarizing the number of photographs within each class is also included. 7. The Photographs: An Analysis and Comparative Study a. An Analysis of the Photographs within the Reports by the Juries b. %+!

! 1. The Great Exhibition of 1851

!

!

The Great Exhibition of the Works of Industry of All Nations (later known as the Great Exhibition, and the Crystal Palace Exhibition) was held in London, England's Hyde Park from May 1 to October 15, 1851. While other national exhibitions of industry and art had been held before10, this was the first international world fair, attracting over six million visitors from around the globe.11 Housed in the impressive Crystal Palace building12, the Great Exhibition of 1851 can be considered a crucial moment in the cultural, scientific, economic and artistic history of Britain.13 While the 1840s saw political and social unrest, the Great Exhibition stimulated a period of economic and social stability in Great Britain.14 Organized under the direction of Prince Albert, the Great Exhibition was an enormous success, positioning Britain as a leader of industrialization, and providing a model for 19th-century international exhibitions that would follow. The exhibition was an extraordinarily large feat: more than 100,000 exhibits filled the Crystal Palace building, sent in by over 14,000 individuals or companies. Over one !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
10

Roger Taylor, "The Great Exhibition of 1851," in Impressed by Light: British Photographs from Paper Negatives, 1840-1860, (New York: The Metropolitan Museum of Art, 2007), 31. France had held national exhibitions since 1798 (in 1798, 1801, 1802, 1806, 1819, 1823, 1827, 1834, 1839, 1844 and 1849). The eleventh National Exposition of the Products of Industry, Agriculture, and Manufacture, held in 1849, was the largest up to that time. 11 Helene Bocard, "The Great Exhibition of the Works of Industry of All Nations, Crystal Palace, Hyde Park (1851)," in Encyclopedia of Nineteenth-Century Photography, ed John Hannavy (New York; London: Routledge, 2008), 614. 12 Hermione Hobhouse, The Crystal Palace and the Great Exhibition: Art, Science, and Productive Industry: A History of the Royal Commission for the Exhibition of 1851 (New York: Athlone Press, 2002), 30-39. The vast Crystal Palace building was erected specifically for the Great Exhibition of 1851, and was designed by Joseph Paxton (1803-1865). It was given its name due to its construction of mostly glass. After the Great Exhibition, the Crystal Palace building was disassembled and moved to a park near Sydenham Hill (London), where it was destroyed by fire on November 30th, 1936. 13 Taylor, "The Great Exhibition of 1851," 31. 14 Bocard, "The Great Exhibition," 614.

!

million objects were on display.15 Described at the time as "vast storehouse of the nations"16, the exhibits ranged from porcelain vases and musical instruments to agricultural tools and raw materials like wool and wood. Photography was also included in the exhibition, which marked an important moment in the industrial and commercial development of the medium.17

Organizing and presenting such a large display of items was a crucial element in the planning process of the exhibition. The Royal Commission, charged with managing all aspects of the event, devised an elaborate classification system consisting of thirty classes18, under four main headings: Raw Materials, Machinery, Manufactures, and Fine Arts.19 However, it was only the British objects that were displayed according to this structure; foreign exhibits (including the British colonies) were grouped by country.20 These classifications would also assist with the appointment of jury members, who had the task of judging the exhibits within each class.21 A committee of notable experts and authorities from around the world was appointed for each class; the committee was responsible for viewing all exhibits within each class, and collectively awarding medals to those exhibits they felt most deserving.22 !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
15 16

Taylor, "The Great Exhibition of 1851," 34. Benjamin Silliman, A Visit to Europe in 1851 (New York: G. P. Putman & Co., 1854), 426. 17 Anthony J. Hamber, A Higher Branch of the Art: Photographing the Fine Arts in England, 1839-1880 (Amsterdam: Gordon and Breach, 1996), 256. 18 For a list of the thirty classes, see section 6 of this finding aid, entitled "Catalogue of the Photographs within the Reports by the Juries". 19 Taylor, "The Great Exhibition of 1851," 35. 20 Ibid. 21 For a description of the medals, see section 6 of this finding aid, entitled "Catalogue of the Photographs within the Reports by the Juries". 22 For instance, Sir David Brewster, notable scientist who was involved in early photography, was appointed chair of the jury for Class X, "Philosophical Instruments and Processes Depending Upon Their Use".

!

!

Over 700 photographic images were displayed at the Great Exhibition. Six countries exhibited photography, but most of the photographic objects came from Britain, the United States and France.23 Fitting photography within the established classification system proved to be problematic, as its status was still quite ambiguous in the early years of its existence.24 Photography did not have its own class, and most of the photographs were included under Class X "Philosophical Instruments and Processes Depending Upon Their Use". Only three photographers were found within Class XXX, Report on Sculpture, Models, and Plastic Art.25 While it not known exactly which photographs were shown, the official catalogues from the Great Exhibition do record the names of the photographers who took part.26 While over six million visitors came to the exhibition, it is unclear how many actually saw the photographic exhibits amidst the thousands of stunning displays. The sheer size of the Crystal Palace meant that visitors were not likely to see all the exhibits in one visit, and photography had to compete for attention amongst other hugely popular items that were on display.27 However, the Great Exhibition of 1851 was a very important event for the history of photography, not only because it was the first time that photographs were shown on a large scale to an international audience,

2. Reports by the Juries: Publication Information Before the Great Exhibition opened, it was agreed that a special publication would be created to present to foreign governments and others who were involved in the organization of the event, as a record and a token of appreciation. For this, it was decided that special editions of the Official Descriptive and Illustrated Catalogue and the Reports by the Juries would be published. The publications were specially printed in large format in eight volumes, and the complete set included the Official Descriptive and Illustrated Catalogue, the Reports by the Juries, the Reports by the Crown, and was accompanied by examples of the exhibition medals that were awarded to the exhibitors. As these presentation sets were intended to reflect Britain's political dominance, no expense was spared to create these luxurious gifts: a leading London bookmaker, Robert Rivière, was commissioned to bind the volumes with red Russian morocco leather and blue silk linings.28 In total, 137 sets were required. 29 As a further enhancement to this special gift, it was decided that the Reports by the Juries would be illustrated with photographs.

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
28 29

Taylor, "The Great Exhibition of 1851," 40. Appendix B of the March 1st, 1852 minutes of the Royal Commission, in the Archive of the Royal Commission for the Exhibition of 1851, indicates that on March 1, 1852, the Royal Commission listed all who were to receive the special presentation set, including: Queen Victoria and Prince Albert (one set each); the 24 members of the Royal Commission; its two secretaries and the Acting Secretary; the five members of the Executive Committee and their secretary; the two Special Commissioners; the chairman of the Finance Committee and the five treasurers; seven members of the Royal Engineers (including Sir William Crossman); the East India Company; the British Museum; the Society of Arts; the School of Design; the cities of Dublin and Edinburgh; the eleven British colonies (including Canada); 43 European countries; the United States; Turkey; the Pasha of Egypt; and Tunis; 9 Indian maharajahs and kings received the Reports by the Juries and the medals, but not the catalogues. W.H.F. Talbot received 15 copies. According to this research by Asser, this adds up to 137 sets. See Asser, "A Handsome and Highly Finished Present," 166 and no. 80. Taylor and Hamber indicate in their texts that 140 sets were produced. See Taylor, "The Great Exhibition of 1851," 40, and Hamber, A Higher Branch, 256.

&'!

!

!

!

It was likely Prince Albert's suggestion to utilize this new medium to illustrate the Reports by the Juries30, rather than chromolithographs, which were at the time commonly used as illustrations.31 It is no surprise that Prince Albert would make such a choice, as he was at the time the president of the Royal Society of Arts and who, along with his wife Queen Victoria, was becoming a well known advocate of photography.32 The production of the photographically illustrated Reports by the Juries was one of the largest and most ambitious projects of its kind to be undertaken up to that time. Over 20,000 photographs were required for the 137 sets, and each photograph had to be printed out individually, processed, and then trimmed and mounted into the publication.33 On top of the magnitude of the work, the complications that arose were to be expected from such an unprecedented project. Photography, at this time being still rather new, was plagued by technical limitations and inconsistent results.34 In addition to this, William Henry Fox Talbot's patent restrictions on the commercial use of his calotype process forced fierce negotiations between himself and the Executive Committee of the Royal Commission, who had commissioned the British calotypist Hugh Owen, who only held an amateur's license to practice the calotype process, to take some of the photographs for the

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
30

Anthony Hamber and Saskia Asser, "Exhibition of the Works of Industry of All Nations, 1851: Reports by the Juries" in Encyclopedia of Nineteenth-Century Photography, ed John Hannavy (New York; London: Routledge, 2008), 507. 31 Publications related to the Great Exhibition of 1851 that were illustrated with lithographs include the Official Descriptive and Illustrated Catalogue, the Art-Journal Illustrated Catalogue: the Industry of all Nations, 1851, and Tallis's History of the Crystal Palace illustrated from steel engravings from daguerreotypes. 32 Frances Dimond and Roger Taylor, Crown and Camera: The Royal Family and Photography, 1842-1910 (Harmondsworth, England: Penguin Books, 1987), 11-25. 33 Hamber and Asser, "Exhibition of the Works of Industry of All Nations, 1851: Reports by the Juries," 507. 34 Hamber, A Higher Branch, 257.

!

!

!

publication.35 This challenge was finally resolved with the offering of fifteen copies of the Reports to Talbot, in exchange for the use of this patented process. The production of the Reports by the Juries was a joint effort by the London paper company Spicer Brothers, and the printing firm of William Clowes and Sons. These two businesses were the most prominent ones in their respective fields in Britain, and the high quality of production is evident. The two firms were jointly responsible for all the printing at the Great Exhibition, holding the rights to produce and sell all official printed material. This was a lucrative deal for the firms, but one that the organizers of the exhibition would not compromise on, as they wanted to ensure that the printed materials were of the highest quality.36 This was an important commission for William Clowes and Sons', as the firm became a recognized authority and was later consulted by organizers of many other exhibitions.37 Although the title page of the publication states the publication date as 1852, the first copies of the Reports by the Juries were not ready for distribution until January 1853, a setback caused by a fire at the printing establishment Clowes and Sons' that destroyed many of the printed texts.38 It wasn't until April 1854 that Talbot received his fifteen copies of the presentation sets.39

The firms were paid a sum of 3200 pounds plus royalties for their duties. 37 Oxford Dictionary of National Biography, s.v. "Clowes, William, the younger." 38 Hamber and Asser, "Exhibition of the Works of Industry of All Nations, 1851: Reports by the Juries," 508 39 Hamber and Asser, "Exhibition of the Works of Industry of All Nations, 1851: Reports by the Juries," 508. 3. Scope and Content: Physical Description of the Publication

The four volume Reports by the Juries, officially entitled The Reports by the Juries on The Subjects in the Thirty Classes into which the Exhibition was Divided, are large leather-bound publications, which form part of a complete eight volume presentation set.40 The entire set in the collection of the Art Gallery of Ontario was acquired along with a specially crafted satinwood and rosewood-banded display cabinet, designed to house the set. Each volume of the Reports measures approximately 35 x 25cm.41 They are bound in red Morocco leather, with gilt lettering on the front and back covers displaying the title, "EXHIBITION MDCCC.LI / REPORTS BY THE / JURIES". The spine also has gilt lettering that reads "EXHIBITION / MDCCC.LI / JURY REPORTS", and the appropriate volume number (i.e. VOL. I, etc.). The front and back cover of each volume is also elaborately stamped in gilt with a V & A (Victoria and Albert) emblem. All the volumes have gilded edges, and the endpapers are of royal blue silk. At the beginning of each volume is a special presentation page, bearing a dedication engraved in red and black, which, in the case of the AGO copy, reads: "Presented by Her Majesty's / Commissioners for the / Exhibition of M.D.CCC.LI. / To William Crossman, / Lieutenant Royal / Engineers."

4. The Photographers and Their Biographies

Hugh Owen (1808-1897) Hugh Owen was born in Bristol, England in 1808, and is considered a master of early photography in Bristol. Very little is known about Owen's early life. He worked as the Chief Cashier for the Great Western Railway, and was a very well connected Bristolian.45 He married twice, and had one daughter. He first began experimenting with photography in the early 1840s, working initially as an amateur daguerreotypist.46 His interest in the calotype process likely began after corresponding with William Henry Fox Talbot, on 4 March 1845. Preparing to give a lecture on photography at the Bristol Mechanics Institute & Philosophical Society, Owen wrote to Talbot seeking examples of the calotype process that he could show as samples "of the beautiful art of which you are the inventor".47 Within a few years, Owen was working exclusively with paper negatives, and became a master of the process. The majority of Owen's surviving photographs are salted paper prints from paper negatives.48 He often left his prints unsigned, but many are inscribed with the letters HO

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
45

J. Belsey, "Art vs. Photography: Hugh Owen's Contribution," The Photographic Journal 136 (April 1996), 180. 46 Larry J, Schaaf and Roger Taylor, "Biographical Dictionary of British Calotypists," in Impressed by Light: British Photographs from Paper Negatives, 1840-1860 (New York: The Metropolitan Museum of Art, 2007), 355. 47 "W.H.F. Talbot to Hugh Owen, 4 March 1845," The Correspondence of William Henry Fox Talbot, accessed May 8, 2011, http://foxtalbot.dmu.ac.uk/letters/letters.html. 48 Lori Pauli, "Owen, Hugh (1808-1897)," in Encyclopedia of Nineteenth-Century Photography, ed. John Hannavy (New York; London: Routledge, 2008), 1041.

on the negatives.49 Many of his photographs depict scenes of Bristol and the surrounding area. Owen was a founding member of the Calotype Club in 1847.50 Owen made thirty-two photographs, for which he is best known, of the exhibits at the Great Exhibition of 1851 that appeared in the publication the Reports by the Juries. Owen's own photographs were entered into competition at the exhibition, and it is likely due to their impressive quality that he was asked to photograph some of the exhibits for the publication.51 The commissioning of Owen to document some of the exhibits for the Reports by the Juries using the paper negative (calotype) process resulted in a debate over the patent restrictions that Talbot had imposed on his invention. Although Owen held an amateur's license from Talbot, he was prohibited from selling the photographs. Owen offered his services for free, but the Commissioners of the Great Exhibition wanted to pay him a fee for his services, and Talbot had to be consulted for his permission.52 In 1854, Owen submitted a letter to the Journal of the Photographic Society that read, in part: "Although I dislike controversy on matters of taste, I must be permitted to remonstrate against the tone assumed by some of the Photographists [sic] of the New School with regard to the paper process...For the delineation of nature, however, I...assert the superiority of paper, both for force and effect, as well as convenience."53 While Owen exhibited photographs up until 1855, including some that were included in

the Reports by the Juries,54 Owen gave up photography around 1856, when the wet collodion on glass process became the dominant photographic process, in part because he disliked the stains left on his fingers.55 Owen later published the book Two Centuries of Ceramic Art in Bristol (1873), and became an expert in the history of Bristol.56

Claude-Marie Ferrier (1811-1889) Claude-Marie Ferrier was born on February 21, 1811 in Lyon, France. Very little is known of his early years. He was married on July 14, 1830, and one son, Jacques Alexandre, was born on September 10, 1831. It is unknown when Ferrier first arrived in Paris, but records indicate that he had begun working with the optician Louis-Jules Duboscq (sometimes referred to as Duboscq-Soleil) before 1851. At this time, Ferrier was likely producing stereographs from daguerreotypes before being instrumental in helping Duboscq successfully create glass stereographs.57 Ferrier's photography is first mentioned in connection with the Great Exhibition of 1851. Along with the calotypist Hugh Owen (see previous entry), Ferrier made 122 photographs of the exhibits at the Great Exhibition that appeared within the Reports by the Juries. Ferrier utilized the albumenized glass-plate negative process, from which Robert Bingham printed salted !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!

It was at this same time that the glass stereograph was invented, and it was at the Great Exhibition that stereomania officially began.58 Claude-Marie Ferrier became one of the founders of the French photographic firm of Ferrier, Soulier, Lévy (FSL), which produced some of the finest glass stereographs in Europe during the late nineteenth century.59 The FSL firm was well known for producing very sharp and high contrast glass stereographs that were achieved using the albumen-on-glass process. Ferrier regularly exhibited his photography in France and England, including some prints from the negatives he created for the Reports by the Juries.60 By 1870, Ferrier's firm had developed into the lantern slide market61, which allowed the enterprise to continue to grow even after stereomania started to decline. Ferrier died on June 14, 1889, but the collection of glass stereographs made by FSL has remained largely intact due to the fact that the company is still in operation today, under the name RogerViollet.62 !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!

! Robert Bingham (1824-1870)

!

Robert Jefferson Bingham was born in Billesdon, Leicestershire, England in 1825. He worked as a chemist, and experimented with photography from the early 1840s.63 He moved to Paris around 1850, where he opened a photographic portrait studio. Bingham claimed to have experimented with the wet collodion process before Frederick Scott Archer's 1851 published announcement of the invention.64 In the 7th edition of his manual Photographic Manipulations, published in 1850, Bingham describes this process,65 and on the verso (back) of many of his carte-de-visites that he produced in Paris is the printed statement "inventeur du procedé collodion".66 Bingham was commissioned to print the salted paper prints for the Reports by the Juries, but it is likely that he only printed Claude-Marie Ferrier's glass plate negatives, and not the paper negatives of Hugh Owen.67 It is unclear how Bingham was chosen for the printing of the photographs in the Reports by the Juries. Issues that arose between the Executive Committee and Talbot, holder of the photographic patent in Britain, complicated the matter68, but in the end, Bingham completed the task in his photographic establishment in Versailles, France.69

Robert Bingham continued to work in France, where he became a leading advocate for the wet collodion process. In the 1850s and 1860s, he specialized in the area of photographic art reproduction.70 He died in 1870 in France.

Nicolass Henneman (1813-1898) Nicolass Henneman was born in Heemskerk, the Netherlands, on December 8th, 1813. At the age of 26, Henneman left the Netherlands and arrived in England in 1838, where he initially worked as Talbot's valet.71 Soon after, Henneman began assisting Talbot with his early photographic experiments, and learnt how to make calotypes and salted paper prints.72 Henneman later ran Talbot's printing enterprise, the Reading Establishment (1844-1847) where he made prints for such publications as Talbot's Pencil of Nature and Sun Pictures of Scotland.73 Henneman opened his own portrait studio at 122 Regent Street in London, with the financial help of Talbot, after the Reading Establishment closed in 1847.74 He worked independently as a commercial photographer, and in 1851, nearly acquired a lucrative contract to print the photographs for the Reports by the Juries publication for the Great Exhibition of 1851. Unfortunately, the Royal Commission, who was in charge of coordinating the production of the jury reports, did not approve of the quality of

5. Photographic Processes Used in the Publication The photographs that appear within the Reports by the Juries are salted paper prints, made from two different negative processes: the paper negative process, and the albumenized glass-plate process. Hugh Owen utilized paper negatives, while ClaudeMarie Ferrier worked with albumenized glass-plate negatives. These two processes are quite different, and they both possess their own unique characteristics. Early photographic processes and formulae were not standardized. Practitioners often experimented with different chemicals and quantities, and success often depended upon location conditions, the skill of the printer, and their knowledge of chemistry. For the purpose of this section, the process descriptions have been taken from Bertrand Lavédrine's Photographs of the Past: Process and Preservation (2009).

Paper Negatives Paper negatives, also called calotypes or talbotypes, were patented in 1841 by William Henry Fox Talbot (British, 1800 - 1877), and refer to a negative image created on a paper support. The term calotype is derived from the Greek word kalos, meaning beautiful. The process of making an early calotype began with a sheet of fine quality writing paper that was coated with a weak solution of silver nitrate. Once dry, it was submerged in a solution of potassium iodide, forming light sensitive silver iodide in the

!

',!

!

!

!

paper fibers.80 To sensitize the paper, a mixture of "gallo-nitrate of silver" was used, which was a solution of silver nitrate, acetic acid and gallic acid.81 The paper was then ready for exposure in the camera, and was exposed while still wet, as this increased the sensitivity. After exposure, the paper was developed in the same solution of gallo-nitrate of silver, rinsed, and "fixed" to stabilize the image. Initially, a solution of potassium bromide was used as a fixer, but it only stabilized the print; later sodium thiosulfate was introduced as a more effective fixing agent to make the negative permanent.82 As a negative, the dark and light tones are reversed, and a positive image was obtained by placing the paper negative in direct contact with another sheet of sensitized paper in sunlight (see Salted Paper Print).83 The benefits of the negative-positive process were not initially acknowledged. The process required more work than its counterpart, the daguerreotype, and it took a number of years for the calotype to become popular.84 This was partly due to Talbot's strict patent, which required a license to utilize his process.85 However, photographers eventually understood the copying potential of the calotype, and even as the sharper and often faster glass negative process was introduced in the 1850s, many continued to use the paper negative process until the end of the decade. Photographers working with the calotype not only realized the benefits of its reproducibility, but they appreciated the !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
80

Roger Taylor, Impressed by Light: British Photographs from Paper Negatives, 18401860 (New York: The Metropolitan Museum of Art, 2007), 4. 81 Ibid. 82 Bertrand Lavédrine, Photographs of the Past: Process and Preservation (Los Angeles: Getty Publications, 2009), 228. 83 Ibid. 84 Lavédrine, Photographs of the Past, 224. 85 See Gail Buckland, Fox Talbot & the Invention of Photography (Boston: D.R. Godine, 1980) for more about Talbot's patent, and Keeler, "Illustrating the `Reports by the Juries'," 257-272, for how this affected the Reports by the Juries.

!

(-!

!

!

!

aesthetic qualities of the paper negative, and those working with this process considered themselves "artists", much in contrast to the commercial goals of those working with the faster glass negative process.86

Albumenized Glass-Plate Negatives Albumenized glass-plate negatives utilize a glass support, rather than paper or metal as was used for the calotype and daguerreotype processes. The process was first published in 1847 by Claude-Félix-Abel Niepce de Saint-Victor in France. In 1849, Louis-Désiré Blanquart-Evrard modified the process, and his became the popular method of creating albumen-on-glass negatives.87 An albumenized glass-plate negative is a negative on a glass plate. The plate was first coated with a solution of albumen from egg whites mixed with water and salt. When dry, it was sensitized by immersion in a solution of silver nitrate and acetic acid: the silver particles are suspended in a layer of albumen, which forms the image upon exposure to light.88 The plate was then rinsed, and exposed in the camera while still wet. After exposure, sometimes up to thirty minutes, the plate was developed using solutions of silver nitrate and gallic acid. Alternating washes in these solutions helped to build up the density to the desired level. Fixing was achieved by using thiosulfate, and finally, the plate was thoroughly washed. Albumenized glass-plate negatives are distinguishable by their ocher-orange or olive green colour. 89 Like the paper negative process, the final !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
86 87

Taylor, Impressed by Light, 70. Ibid, 45. 88 Lavédrine, Photographs of the Past, 235. 89 Ibid.

!

(%!

!

!

!

positive photographic image results from a printing-out procedure (see Salted Paper Print). The use of the glass plate negative resulted in a much sharper image and finer image detail than what the paper negative (see description above) could achieve. Even though glass plate negatives were subject to breakage and the exposures were quite long (sometimes up to thirty minutes), the sharpness and detail that was achieved outweighed these aspects for some photographers. However, this lack of sensitivity meant that the process was used mostly to photograph landscapes or architecture.90 Even as wetcollodion on glass was introduced after 1850, albumen was still favoured by some photographers due to the greater resolution of the process. Albumenized glass-plate negatives remained in use until the 1880s.91

Salted Paper Prints The salted paper print is the earliest silver photographic print process. Salted paper prints are positives made using a negative such as a calotype (paper negative), a waxed paper negative, or a glass plate negative. Prints made from glass negatives often appear much sharper and less grainy than prints made using paper negatives. This can be seen by comparing the prints made from Owen and Ferrier's negatives in the Reports by the Juries.

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
90 91

Lavédrine, Photographs of the Past, 234. Ibid.

!

(&!

!

!

!

Salted paper prints are named this because the first step in preparing the paper support was by treating it with a chloride or "salt".92 To salt the paper, it was immersed or floated it in a solution of salt water (5%). After it had dried, it was sensitized in a solution of silver nitrate (15%), in a darkened room. The paper was now photosensitive, and had to be kept out of the light until it was ready for exposure. When it was dry, the light sensitive paper was "sandwiched" in direct contact with the negative, normally in a printing frame that held the negative and the paper firmly together. This method of contact printing allowed an image to form gradually over time, as the photosensitive material gradually darkened due to the effects of exposure to light and UV radiation. The areas of the negative that were darkest let less light through, making that part of the positive less dense; conversely, the areas of the negative that were lightest received full exposure to the light, forming darker areas on the print.93 Printing was done outside during daylight hours, and the length of the exposure depended on a number of factors, such as the season and weather conditions. Exposure times could vary from a few minutes to many hours.94 A silver image formed on the paper by a photochemical conversion of the silver particles by the exposure to daylight, without the use of a developer, and the image became fully visible entirely by the action of the light.95 The final steps included a fixing bath of sodium thiosulfate, and later, a toning solution of gold salts was used to enhance the tones and make the photograph more stable. Finally,

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
92 93

Richard Farber, Historic Photographic Processes (New York: Alworth Press, 1998), 33. Farber, Historic Photographic Processes, 33. 94 James Reilly, Care and Identification of 19th-Century Photographic Prints (Rochester, NY: Eastman Kodak Company, 1986), 3. 95 Lavendrine, Photographs of the Past, 106.

!

('!

!

!

!

the print was thoroughly washed to remove any remaining compounds, as this could contribute to deterioration.96 Salted paper prints vary in colour from a warm reddish-brown or cold purple-brown (depending on how it is processed, developed or toned with the gold toner), and they always have a matte surface as the prints do not have a binder layer, thus retaining the characteristics of the paper support.97 The image is located within the paper fibers. Salted paper prints were in use through the 1840s and 1850s, and even though the exposure times of the printing out process were lengthy, many preferred the warm tones it produced in comparison to the cooler tones obtained by prints made with a chemical developer.98 By 1855, the popularity of salted paper prints began to fade as the glossy albumen paper process was introduced. However, some photographers continued to utilize the salted paper process after 1855, as they favoured the matte surface characteristics.

Ibid. "Salt Print," The Graphic Atlas, accessed May 6, 2011, http://www.graphicsatlas.org/ Taylor, Impressed by Light, 67.

7. The Photographs: An Analysis and Comparative Study This section is divided into two sub-sections, and provides an analysis and comparative study of the photographs that appear within the Reports by the Juries (Reports). The analysis section focuses on the copy of the Reports at the Art Gallery of Ontario (AGO); and the second, comparative section compares the AGO copy with the one at the Robertson Davies Library, Massey College in the University of Toronto (Robertson Davies Library). This section verifies and extends the research conducted by Asser regarding the photographs that appear within the Reports by the Juries.99 The chapter refers to the photographs found in the catalogue (Appendix B), where more information is found about the photographs.

A: Analysis of the Photographs in the Reports by the Juries The decision to utilize photographs to illustrate such a large-scale publication was unprecedented, and the challenges faced were immense. This section examines what was photographed, where the photographs were taken, the photographer's working methods, and how the photographs were printed and included in the Reports.

What was Photographed The purpose of the Reports by the Juries was to provide a record of the exhibits, and of which exhibitors received an award, and the reports included descriptions of the exhibit items and detailed rationales from the various jury members justifying their !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
99

This analysis and independent comparative study extends research conducted by Saskia Asser in her article "'A Handsome and Highly Finished Present': Foto's voor de juryrapporten van de Great Exhibition, 1851," Bulletin van het Rijksmuseum, 53, no. 2 (2005), 140-77, 229-33.

!

"#!

!

!

!

awards. Spread out amongst the four volumes, alongside the text, are photographs, which document selected exhibits by providing a visual representation of the item that was on display. There are 154 photographs within the AGO's copy of the Reports; five photographs in Volume I; forty-five photographs in Volume II; forty-five photographs in Volume III; and fifty-nine photographs in Volume IV. (The previous chart provides a breakdown of the photographs by class). Of the 154 photographs depicting exhibits at the Great Exhibition, over 60 received a Council Medal, over 50 were awarded a Prize Medal, and several received an Honourable Mention.100 The remaining photographs were either views of the Crystal Palace building (10 photographs), or exhibits that did not appear to have received a medal.101 It is likely that the jury members felt that the best items on display ­ those that were to receive these medals ­ were most worthy of having an illustration within this important publication.102 The volume containing the most photographs is Volume IV. Within this volume, fifty-five of the fifty-nine photographs show sculptural pieces, which appear in Class XXX: Report on Sculpture, Models and Plastic Art. This class contains the most photographs of any class within the Reports. As Hamber notes in his publication A Higher Branch of the Art, this larger number is possibly due to the fact that fine art pieces !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
100

The awards are reported on throughout the Reports by the Juries, and are also all listed by class at the beginning of Volume I, on pages lxxiv to cciv. For some items photographed, it is not clear if an award was received. See page 52 of this finding aid. 101 Several of the photographs by Hugh Owen appear to document an exhibit that did not receive any medal. See "Appendix B: Catalogue Records" within this finding aid. 102 Hamber, A Higher Branch, 259. Besides presenting awards to exhibits within the Great Exhibition, the jury members were also responsible for deciding which exhibits were to be photographed for the Reports by the Juries publication.

!

$%!

!

!

!

such as sculptures were easier to photograph, due to their monochromatic tones.103 Most of these photographs show the light coloured sculpture set in sharp contrast against a dark backdrop (fig. 1). A few examples also depict the reverse, where a dark model is placed against a very light backdrop (fig. 2). An additional reason why there were so many photographs was due to the fact that this part of the Great Exhibition was very popular with audiences. Many publications, which were available to visitors of the exhibition, also contained illustrations of these same sculptures.104 Besides Class XXX, the most photographed classes were: Class VI (Manufacturing Machines and Tools), with 14 photographs; Class X (Philosophical Instruments and Processes depending upon their Use), with 10 photographs; Class XXII (Iron and General Hardware), with 10 photographs; and Class XXIII (Works in Precious Metals, Jewellery, Articles of Virtu, &c.), with 11 photographs. Many of the photographs in the Reports appear adjacent to, or very near to, the text that discusses the object depicted in the photograph and justifies why the item received an award. However, there are exceptions to this. Classes XI to XV contain no photographs of the items within these classes. The photographs that appear in these sections show interior and exterior views of the Crystal Palace building. It is possible that the items within these classes, such as wool, silk, and grains, were not chosen to be photographed because the colours and textures of these materials would have been too difficult to successfully capture with the photographic emulsion.105 As well, Class XVII ("Paper and Stationery, Printing and Bookbinding") and Class VXIII ("Woven, Spun, !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
103 104

Hamber, A Higher Branch, 262. Asser, "A Handsome and Highly Finished Present," 147. 105 Ibid.

!

$&!

!

!

!

Felted and Laid Fabrics, when shown as Specimens of Printing of Dyeing") do not contain any photographs at all, likely for these same reasons. Photographing Methods Hugh Owen and Claude-Marie Ferrier, the photographers assigned the task of documenting the chosen exhibits, photographed most of the objects inside the Crystal Palace building, using only natural light that entered through the glass walls and roof. Many of the objects were set against a cloth backdrop, either light or dark coloured, to isolate the object in the photograph. This is evident in a number of photographs where the creases of the cloth have not been smoothed out (fig. 3). The cloth backdrops were either hung from the steel beams of the Crystal Palace building, or from metal rods ,106 which are visible within the frame of some of the photographs (fig. 4, 5). The photographers arranged smaller objects in front of the backdrop (fig. 6. 7). Larger objects, which could not be moved, were left where they were, and the backdrop was placed behind. This appeared to have been difficult in some instances, where we can see the past the edges of the backdrop, which does not fill the entire frame of the photograph (figs. 4, 5 and 8). However, it was likely intended that in the printing process, the photograph would be cropped so that the backdrop would fill the entire frame of the photograph. However, for some of the subjects, the photographers chose not to use a backdrop at all. In these instances, it is clear that the sheer size of the object being photographed did not permit the use of a backdrop (fig. 9, 10). Some objects were photographed outside, in the grounds of Hyde Park. According to Asser, Ferrier

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
106

Asser, "A Handsome and Highly Finished Present," 157.

!

$'!

!

!

!

requested that the agricultural equipment be brought outside the Crystal Palace.107 These machines were photographed on the west side of the building, using the trees in the park and the popular statue of Richard I (Coeur de Lion) as a backdrop108 (fig 11). While Owen and Ferrier utilized different photographic processes109, the two photographers also possessed their own unique aesthetic for documenting the exhibits. Owen's photographs are identified by the inclusion of his monogram, an "HO", usually visible in the bottom corner of some of his the prints. Owen's photographs are also identifiable due to the differences in image tone, colour, and sharpness, due to the characteristics of the paper negative process. The use of paper negatives resulted in prints that were much softer and less sharp in detail than the photographs from glass-plate negatives, and these differences are very apparent upon a close examination of the prints. Owen's prints are much warmer in tone, while Ferrier's prints have a neutral green or blue tone. In addition to these intrinsic qualities of the negative and the print, Owen's approach to documenting the exhibits at the exhibition appears to be much different than Ferrier's. Owen's use of cloth, which was sometimes accentuated rather than hidden, contrasts with the efforts by Ferrier to disguise the folds of the cloth completely in order to create a neutral background. Owen often arranged pieces of cloth in the foreground of his photographs, likely as a design element (fig. 12). In comparison, Ferrier made every effort to completely isolate the object being photographed, and conceal any use of a !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
107 108

Ibid,160. Asser, "A Handsome and Highly Finished Present," 160. 109 Owen used paper negatives, while Ferrier worked with albumenized glass-plate negatives. See section 5 entitled "Photographic Processes Used in the Publication", and section 4 entitled "The Photographers and their Biographies" within this finding aid.

!

$(!

!

!

!

backdrop, which is especially evident in the photographs of sculptural pieces. In addition, Ferrier's photographs were printed much darker than Owen's, and any trace of backdrop or cloth is often obscured due to the high contrast of the print, and the object looks as though it were floating in space (fig. 13). Moreover, many of Ferrier's negatives have been re-touched, as black boxes are sometimes visible within the print.110 This appears to have been an attempt to remove the exhibitor `s number or object's name, which was affixed to the base of the sculpture or object (fig. 14, 15). As Asser notes in her essay, Ferrier was likely present at the time his negatives were being printed in France, and it is probable that he monitored the printing process.111 The photographs by Owen and Ferrier are also different because of the way they have deteriorated. Most of the photographs by Owen show fading along all the edges of the print, some to a more severe extent than others. In contrast, Ferrier's photographs show very minimal signs of fading. This difference may be the result of the way the negatives were printed. Asser's research suggests that the paper negatives by Owen, and the glass-plate negatives by Ferrier, were not printed by the same person, and therefore, likely were not printed using the same paper or chemical process.112 Additionally, nineteenth-century photography was not a consistent practice: materials were not standardized, and methods of experimentation were constantly being employed throughout the entire process. Asser notes that Bingham likely printed Ferrier's negatives only, and even then, the discrepancies between prints from his same negatives !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
110

For details regarding the touching up and masking out of Ferrier's negatives, see Asser, "A Handsome and Highly Finished Present," 160. 111 Asser, "A Handsome and Highly Finished Present," 164. 112 Early photographic processes were not standardized, and printing processes, methods, and chemical formulations often varied by practitioner.

!

$"!

!

!

!

are evident113 (for more details, see the comparison study below). According to Roger Taylor, the distinctive differences in paper quality and image tone of the prints mirror early British and French prints, and it is therefore likely that Owen's negatives were printed in Britain and not by Robert Bingham.114 It is unclear as to exactly why certain photographs were chosen for the publication, how many negatives were made in total, and if Owen and Ferrier were assigned specific exhibits to photograph. However, it is relevant to note that a larger number of Ferrier's photographs appear within the Reports by the Juries publication: of the 154 photographs, 122 can be assigned to Ferrier, while only thirty-two can be identified as being by Owen. It is not known why this is the case, but it could have been largely due to Owen's attitude towards to his photographic assignment. A letter from the Royal Commission, dated September 24th, 1851 in part reads, The photographs by Mr. Owen do not represent the objects selected by us, but by himself for his own pleasure ­ only some of which we propose to use ­ in fact only those subjects named in the Reports.115

This statement is supported by a comparison of the exhibits that received medals and Owen's photographs: several of the photographs by Owen document objects that did not receive a medal.116

Asser, "A Handsome and Highly Finished Present," 166. Taylor, "The Great Exhibition of 1851," 42 and no. 77. 115 Hamber, A Higher Branch, 261. 116 Such as "Large Anchor, Brown, Lenox and Co." (Catalogue Vol. III, no. 7) and "Ivory Casket, Matifat" (Catalogue Vol. III, no. 45).

B. Comparison Study Between a Set of the Reports by the Juries at the Robertson Davies Library, Massey College in the University of Toronto and the Set at the Art Gallery of Ontario In comparing the AGO copy of the Reports by the Juries, with the one at the Robertson Davies Library, it becomes clear how each copy of the Reports is in some ways unique. This is largely the result of the difficulties faced by early practitioners of photography, especially when working on such a large-scale project as the Reports. The Robertson Davies Library's set of the Reports was presented to the British architect Sir Charles Barry (1795-1860), who served as a member of the Royal Commission for the Great Exhibition of 1851.

Sequencing Differences The first difference between the two copies lies in the sequencing of the photographs. Volume III of the Robertson Davies Library's copy, for example, contains forty-four photographs, while volume III of the AGO's copy contains forty-five photographs. The last photograph, Ivory Casket, Matifat, is absent from volume III of the Robertson Davies Library's copy. I initially suspected that this photograph was missing altogether, as it is intended to be the last photograph within volume III.117 However, this photograph is, in fact, reproduced in volume IV, where it wrongly appears within Class XXIX (facing page 1338). Additionally, other sequencing differences are apparent. Volume II, to take another example, contains two photographs of an exhibit by "Hibbert, Platt and Sons" (see page 411 and 414 of the Reports by the Juries). Within these two !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
117

According to the List of Illustrations at the beginning of volume III, the photograph Ivory Casket, Matifat is meant to be included in this volume, and is listed as the last photograph within volume III (facing page 1322).

!

$)!

!

!

!

publications, the order of the photographs is reversed,118 and the two images appear in a different sequence. This is likely the result of human error when the photographs were mounted. These initial findings attest to the hand-made nature of early photographically illustrated publications, and reflect the hardships faced in completing such a large project within a very short period.119

Printing and Cropping the Prints The task of completing the Reports by the Juries required the printing of a total of over 20,000 photographs.120 This was a very large undertaking. As Asser notes in her article, Robert Bingham was able to print about eighty prints per day, and even if he printed only Ferrier's negatives, he was required to print approximately 16, 858 prints in total.121 The haste of this task, with all of the time consuming steps involved, is evident in a comparison of prints, which were made from the same negative, in the two sets. Differences in contrast and tone are evident between the photographs, and further evidence of fingerprints or chemical stains can be seen in one photograph and not the other (fig.16, 17). !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
118

In the AGO's copy of the Reports, the photograph "Cotton Machinery, Hibbert, Platt and Sons" (Volume II, Catalogue 7) appears opposite page 411, and the photograph "Cotton Machinery, Hibbert, Platt and Sons (2)" (Volume II, Catalogue 8) appears opposite page 414. In the Robertson Davies Library copy, the photograph "Cotton Machinery, Hibbert, Platt and Sons" (Volume II, Catalogue 7) appears opposite page 414, and the photograph "Cotton Machinery, Hibbert, Platt and Sons (2)" (Volume II, Catalogue 8) appears opposite page 411. 119 Differences in photographic sequencing are not the only inaccuracies that arose during publication. Errors in pagination are evident: in both copies of Volume I, the page number printed on page 358 reads "853", and similarly, page "8128" should be page 1828. A review of the online catalog records for the Reports by the Juries in other institutions reveals this error exists in most copies. 120 137 copies (122 plus fifteen to Talbot), with 154 photographs, would equal 21, 098 photographs. 121 Asser, "A Handsome and Highly Finished Present," 164.

!

$*!

!

!

!

Once the photographs were printed, they each had to be cropped, or trimmed, before they were glued onto the pages, which were then inserted into the publication. 19th-century photographic prints required this step of trimming; the negatives were normally larger than the print, and had to be cropped to remove any blemishes from the negative that appeared around the edges. Differences in cropping are very common throughout the Reports. An examination, for example, of Owens' photograph "Large Blocks of Coal" reveals significant cropping differences. Although both the AGO copy and the Robertson Davies Library copy are virtually the same size (17.3 x 22.6 cm and 17.3 x 22.5 cm, respectively) the cropping is not identical, as the AGO copy shows slightly less sky than the Robertson Davies Library copy, and the sides of the prints show different information. This is significant for this study because the examination of this slight difference in cropping uncovers Owen's monogram ("HO") in the Robertson Davies Library print, which only showed a fragment of the monogram in the AGO version of the print (fig. 18, 19, 20, 21), Different croppings can also be found in other prints within the publications, and helps to establish a positive attribution of Owen's photographs, where his monogram may not be evident in one copy, but can be found in the other.

Quality of the Prints The details of the photographic printing processes employed to print the photographs for the Reports by the Juries is not known, such as the specific formula for the developer or the fix, but it is clear that two separate printing methods were

!

$#!

!

!

!

employed.122 Owen's prints vary considerably in appearance when compared with the prints made from Ferrier's negatives, and although this is partly the result of the different negative processes, an examination of the way the prints have faded is revealing. The Owen prints show consistent (and quite significant) signs of fading around the edges, while Ferrier's prints have held up well, showing very minimal signs of edge fading. The comparison between the AGO's and Robertson Davies Library's copies of the Reports also brings to light issues of the preservation of nineteenth-century photographs. While it is unknown what kind of environment these two sets were kept in since they were created, a comparison of the photographs suggests that the AGO copy was kept in a more suitable and stable environment. The photographs in the Robertson Davies Library's copy are generally much more faded, likely the result of higher storage temperatures and higher relative humidity (fig. 22, 23). In addition, it appears as though the Robertson Davies Library copy has been handled more frequently, as signs of wear are evident throughout, as well as on the exterior of the volumes.

Different Negatives and Different Croppings A number of variant photographs exist within the copies of the Reports by the Juries. The photograph of Fromont and Sons' Turbine ("Turbine, Fromont"), found in Volume II, opposite page 368, for example, was photographed at least twice. This photograph in the AGO's copy and the one in the copy at the Robertson Davies Library were printed from two different negatives. This is clear from a very close examination of !!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
122

Early photographic processes were not standardized, and varied from one practitioner to the next.

!

+%!

!

!

!

the two prints, where it is evident that the camera has been moved slightly for the alternate negative (fig. 24, 25). A number of other instances have been identified by Asser123, and have also been confirmed in these two copies, including "Glycera, Wyatt", and "Cain and his Family, Etex" in Volume IV (fig.26, 27). In particular, "Glycera, Wyatt" shows a rather extreme difference in the camera position between the two alternate negatives. The two negatives show completely different views of the sculpture, where most of the other variant negatives show only a slight change in the camera position (fig. 28, 29). Photographers working at this time commonly made more than one negative for each shot, for use when printing (particularly when many prints were required) or to ensure that a successful negative was obtained.

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
123

8. Provenance The copy of the Reports in the collection of the Art Gallery of Ontario originally belonged to Major General Sir William Crossman K.C.M.G. (1830-1901) of the Royal Engineers. Crossman was born on June 30, 1830 in Middlesex, England. He enrolled at the Royal Military Academy in January 1847, and by 1848 he was commissioned as second lieutenant in the Royal Engineers.124 The Royal Engineers were a branch of the British Military to provide technical expertise and engineering tasks, and played an important role in the organization and management of the Great Exhibition.125 From December 1st, 1850 to September 8, 1851, Crossman was responsible for various duties, including determining space and layout for the exhibition space in the Crystal Palace, assisting with the ventilation system, and acting as Assistant Secretary to the Department of Juries, Group B ("Machinery").126 The year after the Great Exhibition, Crossman was sent to Australia, where he worked as superintendent on the construction of facilities by convicts. He became a police magistrate, and in February 1854, he was promoted to lieutenant.127 Crossman married Catherine Josephine in March 1855, and they had two sons and three daughters. In December 1861, he was sent to the colony of Canada where he assisted with military

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
124 125

Oxford Dictionary of National Biography, s.v. "Crossman, Sir William (1830-1901)." Members of the Royal Engineers were specifically responsible for assisting with the production of the Reports by the Juries, being commissioned to assist with gluing the photographs into each of the 137 volumes. See Hamber. A Higher Branch, 263. 126 "Sale 9202/Lot 190." 127 Oxford Dictionary of National Biography, s.v. "Crossman, Sir William (1830-1901)."

!

+(!

!

!

!

preparations for the Trent Affair.128 After this, he traveled extensively across the nation as a secretary to the Royal Commission on the defenses of Canada.129 Crossman worked in many posts overseas, visiting various British colonies (especially in the Far East) in a military capacity. In 1884 he was knighted as K.C.M.G.130 He was promoted to colonel on May 6, 1885 but resigned his command to stand for parliament. He was elected a Liberal MP for Portsmouth in June 1885.131 Crossman retired from the army on January 6, 1886, with the honourary rank of MajorGeneral. He served as sheriff of Northumberland County from 1894-5. He married again in 1899. William Crossman died in London on April 19th, 1901, survived by his second wife, Annie.132 The presentation set stayed within his family after his death until October 2001, when it was put up for auction at Christie's auction house in London, England.133 David Thomson, a prominent Canadian art and photography collector, acquired the set, including the cabinet, at this auction, and in 2007, he donated it to the AGO. In the

The Trent Affair was a diplomatic crisis that arose during the American Civil War, between Britain the United States. Canada was situated as a possible battleground, but the crisis passed. Canadian leaders recognized Canada's vulnerable position and a commission was established on how to best defend the colony Canada. 129 Oxford Dictionary of National Biography, s.v. "Crossman, Sir William (1830-1901)." 130 Knight Commander (K.C.M.G.) is awarded to those who render extraordinary non-military foreign service, or for loyal service to Commonwealth affairs. 131 As a Liberal Unionist, Crossman retained his seat until 1892. 132 Ibid. 133 "Sale 9202/Lot 190."

9. Preliminary List of Copies of the Reports by the Juries in Other Public Institutions The complete presentation set from the Great Exhibition of 1851 was presented to individuals who had helped organize the exhibition, selected British institutions, and internationally to governments of those countries that had participated in the Great Exhibition, including British colonies.135 While the complete set, including the photographically-illustrated Reports by the Juries, is rare, a number of copies can be found in public institutions throughout the world. The following chart lists those public institutions that hold a copy of the fourvolume Reports by the Juries illustrated with salted paper prints. It includes, from left to right, the name and location of the institution that holds the publication, the number of volumes in their collection, and where possible, the inscription on the special presentation page.136 This list is not exhaustive and contains as much information as I was able to gather at the time of completing this project (August 2011). It does not include private collectors who hold copies.137

APPENDIX B: FULL CATALOGUE

!

'#!

!

!

!

Introduction to the Catalogue of Photographs

This is a complete catalogue of all the 154 photographs appearing in the Reports by the Juries publication that is in the collection of the Art Gallery of Ontario. The catalogue has been divided into four sections, in accordance with each of the four volumes of the Reports by the Juries: Volume I (containing 5 photographs); Volume II (containing 45 photographs); Volume III (containing 45 photographs); and Volume IV (containing 59 photographs). The catalogue includes a thumbnail of each photograph, and contains thirteen fields, which are defined as follows:

1. Title This is title assigned by the cataloguer; it is based on, but not identical to, the printed inscription as it appears on the mount below each photograph and in the List of Illustrations at the beginning of each volume of the Reports. 2. Photographer This field indicates the name of the photographer who made the negative, which is either Hugh Owen, or Claude-Marie Ferrier. 3. Negative Date The negative date is the date that the photographer created the negative. Since it is not known precisely when the negatives were taken during the Great Exhibition, which ran from May 1 to October 15, 1851, this is given as May to October 1851.

!

'%!

! 4. Print Date

!

!

This field indicates the date that the salted paper print was made that appears within the Reports by the Juries. It is unclear exactly when the photographs were printed, but they are documented as having been printed between late 1851 (after the exhibition closed) and 1852 (when the first bound copies were produced) 5. Medium This field indicates the photographic process of the print; in this case being "salted paper print". 6. Dimensions Image: This field records the dimensions of the photograph. Mount: This field records the dimensions of the support that the photographic image has been adhered to; in this case, it is the leaf or page that has been inserted into the publication. These dimensions are given in centimetres with height preceding width. 7. Location in Reports This field indicates where the photograph is located within the Reports by the Juries with reference to: a) Volume: The volume where the photograph is found (I, II, III or IV) b) Class: The class to which the photograph belongs (Class I to XXX) c) Page Number: The page that the photograph faces or follows 8. Inscriptions This field records hand written notations and printed texts that appear on the front (recto) of the photograph and on its mount. Note: The back (verso) of the photograph is not accessible.

!

'&!

! 10. Notes

!

!

This field provides relevant information about the photograph that is not contained in any other field.138 11. No. in Catalogue This field records the exhibit number of the item that has been photographed, as it appears in the Official Illustrated and Descriptive Catalogue, which accompanies the Reports by the Juries. 12. Award This field records the award that the exhibitor received for the item that has been photographed, if applicable. The jury members granted awards for the best items on display at the Great Exhibition. These awards were Council Medal, Prize Medal, and Honourable Mention. 13. AGO Accession # This field records the unique object number given to the photograph by the Art Gallery of Ontario.

!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!
138

Information about photographs exhibited following the Great Exhibition of 1851 is found in Taylor, Photographs Exhibited in Britain 1839-1865, 343-346; 565-569, and refers to a photograph made from the same negative as the ones used for the photographs in the Reports by the Juries. The exhibition name and year is noted, as well as the catalogue number of the photograph as it appeared in that exhibition's catalogue.
